     <p><b>Washington University in St. Louis</b> (<b>Wash. U.</b>, or <b>WUSTL</b>) is a private <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">research university</a> located in suburban <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Founded in 1853, and named after <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, the university has students and faculty from all 50 <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> and more than 120 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-enrollment_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enrollment-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Twenty-two <a href="/wiki/Nobel_laureates" title="Nobel laureates" class="mw-redirect">Nobel laureates</a> have been affiliated with Washington University, nine having done the major part of their pioneering research at the university.<sup id="cite_ref-faculty09_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faculty09-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Washington University's undergraduate program is ranked 14th in the nation and 7th in admissions selectivity by <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_and_World_Report" title="U.S. News and World Report" class="mw-redirect">U.S. News and World Report</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-endowment_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-endowment-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is ranked 30th in the world by the <a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2006, the university received $434 million in federal research funds, ranking seventh among private universities receiving federal research and development support, and in the top four in funding from the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Washington University is made up of seven <a href="/wiki/Graduate_school" title="Graduate school">graduate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate_education" title="Undergraduate education">undergraduate schools</a> that encompass a broad range of <a href="/wiki/List_of_academic_disciplines" title="List of academic disciplines">academic fields</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Officially incorporated as &quot;The Washington University,&quot; the university is occasionally referred to as &quot;WUSTL,&quot; an acronym derived from its initials. More commonly, however, students refer to the university as &quot;Wash. U.&quot; To prevent confusion over its location, the <a href="/wiki/Trustee" title="Trustee">Board of Trustees</a> added the phrase &quot;in St. Louis&quot; in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-george_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-george-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

            <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Recent_history_.281955.E2.80.93present.29"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Recent history (1955–present)</span></a></li>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history_.281853.E2.80.931900.29">Early history (1853–1900)</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history (1853–1900)">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Washington University was conceived by 17 <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis</a> business, political, and religious leaders concerned by the lack of institutions of higher learning in the <a href="/wiki/Midwest" title="Midwest" class="mw-redirect">Midwest</a>. Missouri State Senator <a href="/wiki/Wayman_Crow" title="Wayman Crow">Wayman Crow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> minister <a href="/wiki/William_Greenleaf_Eliot" title="William Greenleaf Eliot">William Greenleaf Eliot</a>, grandfather of the poet <a href="/wiki/T.S._Eliot" title="T.S. Eliot" class="mw-redirect">T.S. Eliot</a>, led the effort.</p> 
     <p>The university's first chancellor was <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Gibson_Hoyt" title="Joseph Gibson Hoyt">Joseph Gibson Hoyt</a>. Crow secured the university charter from the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_General_Assembly" title="Missouri General Assembly">Missouri General Assembly</a> in 1853, and Eliot was named President of the Board of Trustees. Early on, Eliot solicited support from members of the local business community, including <a href="/wiki/John_O%27Fallon" title="John O'Fallon">John O'Fallon</a>, but Eliot failed to secure a permanent endowment. Washington University is unusual among major American universities in not having had a prior financial endowment. The institution had no backing of a religious organization, single wealthy patron, or earmarked government support.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:WGEliot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/WGEliot.jpg/150px-WGEliot.jpg" width="150" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/WGEliot.jpg/225px-WGEliot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/WGEliot.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:WGEliot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> William Greenleaf Eliot, first president of the Board of Trustees
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>During the three years following its inception, the university bore three different names. The board first approved &quot;Eliot Seminary,&quot; but William Eliot was uncomfortable with naming a university after himself and objected to the establishment of a seminary, which would implicitly be charged with teaching a religious faith. He favored a nonsectarian university.<sup id="cite_ref-founding_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-founding-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1854, the Board of Trustees changed the name to &quot;Washington Institute&quot; in honor of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>. Naming the University after the nation's first president, only seven years before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> and during a time of bitter national division, was no coincidence. During this time of conflict, Americans universally admired George Washington as the father of the United States and a symbol of national unity. The Board of Trustees believed that the university should be a force of unity in a strongly divided Missouri. In 1856, the University amended its name to &quot;Washington University.&quot; The university amended its name once more in 1976, when the Board of Trustees voted to add the suffix &quot;in St. Louis&quot; to distinguish the university from the nearly two dozen other universities bearing Washington's name.<sup id="cite_ref-george_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-george-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Although chartered as a university, for many years Washington University functioned primarily as a night school located on 17th Street and Washington Avenue in the heart of downtown St. Louis. Owing to limited financial resources, Washington University initially used public buildings. Classes began on October 22, 1854, at the Benton School building. At first the university paid for the evening classes, but as their popularity grew, their funding was transferred to the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Public_Schools" title="St. Louis Public Schools">St. Louis Public Schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Eventually the board secured funds for the construction of Academic Hall and a half dozen other buildings. Later the university divided into three departments: the Manual Training School, <a href="/wiki/Smith_Academy" title="Smith Academy">Smith Academy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mary_Institute" title="Mary Institute" class="mw-redirect">Mary Institute</a>.</p> 
     <p>In 1867, the university opened the first private nonsectarian <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_School_of_Law" title="Washington University School of Law">law school</a> west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>. By 1882, Washington University had expanded to numerous departments, which were housed in various buildings across St. Louis. Medical classes were first held at Washington University in 1891 after the St. Louis Medical College decided to affiliate with the University, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Washington University School of Medicine">School of Medicine</a>. During the 1890s, <a href="/wiki/Robert_S._Brookings" title="Robert S. Brookings">Robert Sommers Brookings</a>, the president of the Board of Trustees, undertook the tasks of reorganizing the university's finances, putting them onto a sound foundation, and buying land for a new campus.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era_.281900.E2.80.931955.29">Modern era (1900–1955)</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Modern era (1900–1955)">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:WUFranGat.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/WUFranGat.JPG/220px-WUFranGat.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/WUFranGat.JPG/330px-WUFranGat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/WUFranGat.JPG/440px-WUFranGat.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:WUFranGat.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Washington University crest at the entrance to 
        <a href="/wiki/Francis_Field_(St._Louis)" title="Francis Field (St. Louis)" class="mw-redirect">Francis Field</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Washington University spent its first half century in downtown St. Louis bounded by Washington Ave., Lucas Place, and Locust Street. By the 1890s, owing to the dramatic expansion of the Manual School and a new benefactor in Robert Brookings, the University began to move west. The University Board of Directors began a process to find suitable ground and hired the landscape architecture firm <a href="/wiki/Olmsted_Brothers" title="Olmsted Brothers">Olmsted, Olmsted &amp; Eliot</a> of Boston. A committee of Robert S. Brookings, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ware_Eliot" title="Henry Ware Eliot">Henry Ware Eliot</a>, and William Huse found a site of 103 acres (41.7&nbsp;ha) just beyond <a href="/wiki/Forest_Park_(St._Louis)" title="Forest Park (St. Louis)">Forest Park</a>, located west of the city limits in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_County,_Missouri" title="St. Louis County, Missouri">St. Louis County</a>. The elevation of the land was thought to resemble the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_of_Athens" title="Acropolis of Athens">Acropolis</a> and inspired the nickname of &quot;Hilltop&quot; campus, renamed the Danforth campus in 2006 to honor former chancellor <a href="/wiki/William_H._Danforth" title="William H. Danforth">William H. Danforth</a>.</p> 
     <p>In 1899, the university opened a national design contest for the new campus. The renowned Philadelphia firm <a href="/wiki/Cope_%26_Stewardson" title="Cope &amp; Stewardson" class="mw-redirect">Cope &amp; Stewardson</a> won unanimously with its plan for a row of <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_Gothic" title="Collegiate Gothic" class="mw-redirect">Collegiate Gothic</a> quadrangles inspired by <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> Universities.<sup id="cite_ref-Architecture_of_Danforth_Campus_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Architecture_of_Danforth_Campus-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The cornerstone of the first building, Busch Hall, was laid on October 20, 1900. The construction of <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Hall" title="Brookings Hall">Brookings Hall</a>, Ridgley, and Cupples began shortly thereafter. The school delayed occupying these buildings until 1905 to accommodate the <a href="/wiki/1904_World%27s_Fair" title="1904 World's Fair" class="mw-redirect">1904 World's Fair</a> and Olympics. The delay allowed the university to construct ten buildings instead of the seven originally planned. This original cluster of buildings set a precedent for the development of the Danforth Campus; Cope &amp; Stewardson’s original plan and its choice of building materials have, with few exceptions, guided the construction and expansion of the Danforth Campus to the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-Architecture_of_Danforth_Campus_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Architecture_of_Danforth_Campus-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Brookings.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Brookings.jpg/220px-Brookings.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Brookings.jpg/330px-Brookings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Brookings.jpg/440px-Brookings.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Brookings.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Hall" title="Brookings Hall">Brookings Hall</a> Quad
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>By 1915, construction of a new medical complex was completed on Kingshighway in what is now St. Louis’s Central West End. Three years later, Washington University admitted its first women medical students.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1922, a young physics professor, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Holly_Compton" title="Arthur Holly Compton" class="mw-redirect">Arthur Holly Compton</a>, conducted a series of experiments in the basement of Eads Hall that demonstrated the &quot;particle&quot; concept of electromagnetic radiation. Compton’s discovery, known as the “<a href="/wiki/Compton_Effect" title="Compton Effect" class="mw-redirect">Compton Effect</a>,” earned him the Nobel Prize in physics in 1927.</p> 
     <p>During World War II, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a> at Washington University was used to produce small quantities of the newly discovered element <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> via <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> bombardment of <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_nitrate" title="Uranyl nitrate">uranium nitrate hexahydrate</a>. The plutonium produced there in 1942 was shipped to the <a href="/wiki/Metallurgical_Laboratory" title="Metallurgical Laboratory">Metallurgical Laboratory</a> Compton had established at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> where <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Seaborg" title="Glenn Seaborg" class="mw-redirect">Glenn Seaborg</a>'s team used it for extraction, purification, and characterization studies of the exotic substance.</p> 
     <p>After working for many years at the University of Chicago, Arthur Holly Compton returned to St. Louis in 1946 to serve as Washington University's ninth chancellor. Compton reestablished the Washington University football team, making the declaration that athletics were to be henceforth played on a &quot;strictly amateur&quot; basis with no athletic scholarships. Under Compton’s leadership, enrollment at the University grew dramatically, fueled primarily by World War&nbsp;II veterans' use of their GI Bill benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1947, <a href="/wiki/Gerty_Cori" title="Gerty Cori">Gerty Cori</a>, a professor at the School of Medicine, became the first American woman to win a Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine. Professors Carl and Gerty Cori became Washington University's fifth and sixth Nobel laureates for their discovery of how glycogen is broken down and resynthesized in the body.</p> 
     <p>The process of desegregation at Washington University began in 1947 with the School of Medicine and the School of Social Work.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> During the mid and late 1940s, the University was the target of critical editorials in the local African American press, letter-writing campaigns by churches and the local <a href="/wiki/Urban_League" title="Urban League" class="mw-redirect">Urban League</a>, and legal briefs by the NAACP intended to strip its tax-exempt status. In spring 1949, a Washington University student group, the Student Committee for the Admission of Negroes (SCAN), began campaigning for full racial integration. In May 1952, the Board of Trustees passed a resolution desegregating the school's undergraduate divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history_.281955.E2.80.93present.29">Recent history (1955–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Recent history (1955–present)">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG/220px-WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG/330px-WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG/440px-WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:WashU_Graham_Chapel.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Graham Chapel
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>During the latter half of the 20th century, Washington University transitioned from a strong regional university to a national research institution. In 1957, planning began for the construction of the “South 40,” a complex of modern residential halls. With the additional on-campus housing, Washington University, which had been predominately a “streetcar college” of commuter students, began to attract a more national pool of applicants.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1964, over two-thirds of incoming students came from outside the St. Louis area.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1971, the Board of Trustees appointed Chancellor <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Danforth" title="William Henry Danforth">William Henry Danforth</a>, who guided the university through the social and financial crises of the 1970s and strengthened the university’s often strained relationship with the St. Louis community. During his 24-year chancellorship, Danforth significantly improved the <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Washington University School of Medicine">School of Medicine</a>, established 70 new faculty chairs, secured a $1.72 billion endowment, and tripled the amount of student scholarships.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Mark_S._Wrighton" title="Mark S. Wrighton">Mark S. Wrighton</a>, former Provost at <a href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT" class="mw-redirect">MIT</a>, was elected the university’s 14th chancellor. During Chancellor Wrighton's tenure undergraduate applications to Washington University have more than doubled. Since 1995, the University has added more than 190 endowed professorships, revamped its Arts &amp; Sciences curriculum, and completed more than 30 new buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The growth of Washington University’s reputation has coincided with a series of record-breaking fund-raising efforts during the last three decades. From 1983 to 1987, the “Alliance for Washington University” campaign raised $630.5 million, which was then the most successful fund-raising effort in national history.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> From 1998 to 2004, the “Campaign for Washington University” raised $1.55 billion, which has been applied to additional scholarships, professorships, and research initiatives.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Presidential_and_Vice_Presidential_debates">U.S. Presidential and Vice Presidential debates</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: U.S. Presidential and Vice Presidential debates">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Wustldebate08.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/Wustldebate08.JPG/220px-Wustldebate08.JPG" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/Wustldebate08.JPG/330px-Wustldebate08.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/Wustldebate08.JPG/440px-Wustldebate08.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Wustldebate08.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/2008_Vice_Presidential_Debate" title="2008 Vice Presidential Debate" class="mw-redirect">2008 Vice Presidential Debate</a> at the Washington University Field House
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Washington University has been selected by the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Presidential_Debates" title="Commission on Presidential Debates">Commission on Presidential Debates</a> to host more presidential and vice presidential debates than any other institution in history.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_debates" title="United States presidential election debates">United States presidential election debates</a> were held at the Washington University Athletic Complex in 1992, 2000, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_debates,_2004#Second_presidential_debate_.E2.80.94_October_8" title="United States presidential election debates, 2004">2004</a>. A presidential debate was planned to occur in 1996, but owing to scheduling difficulties between the candidates, the debate was canceled.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The university hosted the only 2008 vice presidential debate, between <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(US)" title="Republican Party (US)" class="mw-redirect">Republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Palin" title="Sarah Palin">Sarah Palin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(US)" title="Democratic Party (US)" class="mw-redirect">Democrat</a> <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a>, on October 2, 2008, also at the Washington University Athletic Complex.</p> 
     <p>Although Chancellor Wrighton had noted after the 2004 debate that it would be &quot;improbable&quot; that the University will host another debate and was not eager to commit to the possibility,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> he subsequently changed his view and the University submitted a bid for the 2008 debates. &quot;These one-of-a-kind events are great experiences for our students, they contribute to a national understanding of important issues, and they allow us to help bring national and international attention to the St. Louis region as one of America's great metropolitan areas,&quot; said Wrighton.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings_and_reputation">Rankings and reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rankings and reputation">edit</a>]</span></h2>

     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:SeigleHall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/SeigleHall.jpg/220px-SeigleHall.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/SeigleHall.jpg/330px-SeigleHall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/SeigleHall.jpg/440px-SeigleHall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:SeigleHall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Seigle Hall, shared by the 
        <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_School_of_Law" title="Washington University School of Law">School of Law</a> and the College of Arts and Sciences
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Washington University's undergraduate program is ranked 14th in the nation, and 7th in admissions selectivity, in the 2013 <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> National Universities ranking.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, 19 undergraduate disciplines are ranked among the top 10 programs in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2013, Washington University received a record 30,117 applications for a freshman class of 1,500 with an acceptance rate of 13.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> More than 90% of incoming freshmen were ranked in the top 10% of their high school classes. In 2006, the University ranked fourth overall and second among private universities in the number of enrolled <a href="/wiki/National_Merit_Scholar" title="National Merit Scholar" class="mw-redirect">National Merit Scholar</a> freshmen, according to the National Merit Scholar Corp.'s annual report.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2008, Washington University was ranked number one for quality of life according to the <a href="/wiki/Princeton_Review" title="Princeton Review" class="mw-redirect">Princeton Review</a>, among other top rankings. In addition, the Olin Business School's undergraduate program is among the top 4 in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> The Olin Business School's undergraduate program is also among the country's most competitive, admitting only 14% of applicants in 2007 and ranking No. 1 in SAT scores with an average composite of 1492 M+CR according to <a href="/wiki/BusinessWeek" title="BusinessWeek" class="mw-redirect">BusinessWeek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Graduate schools include the <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Washington University School of Medicine">School of Medicine</a>, currently ranked 6th in the nation, and the <a href="/wiki/George_Warren_Brown_School_of_Social_Work" title="George Warren Brown School of Social Work">George Warren Brown School of Social Work</a>, currently ranked 1st. The program in occupational therapy at Washington University currently occupies the second spot for the U.S. News and World Report rankings, and the program in physical therapy is ranked 3rd (tied for #3). For the 2013 edition, the <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_School_of_Law" title="Washington University School of Law">School of Law</a> is ranked 19th and the <a href="/wiki/Olin_Business_School" title="Olin Business School">Olin Business School</a> is ranked 21st.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally, the Graduate School of Architecture and Urban Design was ranked 9th in the nation by the journal <i>DesignIntelligence</i> in its 2013 edition of &quot;America's Best Architecture &amp; Design Schools.&quot;</p> 
     <p>Global rankings include 31st in the <a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a> by <a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Jiao_Tong_University" title="Shanghai Jiao Tong University">Shanghai Jiao Tong University</a> in 2012, that assesses the quality of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific research</a> leading toward a Nobel Prize.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2010, Washington University ranked 38th in the world according to the <a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education_World_University_Rankings" title="Times Higher Education World University Rankings">Times Higher Education World University Rankings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Washington University has been criticized for aggressively marketing itself in order to increase applications and thereby selectivity, a factor in many ranking systems.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_.26_campus">Geography &amp; campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography &amp; campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <p>Although the school includes St. Louis in its name, the majority of the school's main campus (including Brookings Hall) is located in unincorporated St. Louis County and suburban Clayton.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_Campus">Medical Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Medical Campus">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Washington University Medical Center comprises 164 acres (66.4&nbsp;ha) spread over approximately 12 city blocks, located along the eastern edge of <a href="/wiki/Forest_Park_(St._Louis)" title="Forest Park (St. Louis)">Forest Park</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Central_West_End" title="Central West End" class="mw-redirect">Central West End</a> neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis</a>. The campus is home to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Washington University School of Medicine">Washington University School of Medicine</a> and its associated teaching hospitals, <a href="/wiki/Barnes-Jewish_Hospital" title="Barnes-Jewish Hospital">Barnes-Jewish Hospital</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Children%27s_Hospital" title="St. Louis Children's Hospital">St. Louis Children's Hospital</a>. Many of the buildings are connected via a series of <a href="/wiki/Skyway" title="Skyway">skyways</a> and corridors.</p> 
     <p>The School's 2,100 employed and volunteer faculty Physicians &amp; Nurse Practitioners also serve as the medical staff of Barnes-Jewish and St. Louis Children's hospitals, which are part of BJC HealthCare. Washington University and BJC have taken on many joint venture projects, such as the Center for Advanced Medicine, completed in December 2001. BJC Institute of Health at Washington University is the newest research building with 680,000 square feet (63,000&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>).</p> 
     <p>Olin <a href="/wiki/Dormitory" title="Dormitory">Residence Hall</a>, named for Spencer T. Olin, provides residential services for 200 medical and graduate students.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Medical Campus is accessible via the <a href="/wiki/Central_West_End_(St._Louis_MetroLink)" title="Central West End (St. Louis MetroLink)">Central West End MetroLink station</a>, which provides a quick link to the Danforth, North, and West Campuses.</p> 
     <p>Medical Campus Includes:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Barnes-Jewish_Hospital" title="Barnes-Jewish Hospital">Barnes-Jewish Hospital</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Central_Institute_for_the_Deaf" title="Central Institute for the Deaf">Central Institute for the Deaf</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Children%27s_Hospital" title="St. Louis Children's Hospital">St. Louis Children's Hospital</a></li> 
      <li>Rehabilitation Institute of St. Louis</li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Alvin_J._Siteman_Cancer_Center" title="Alvin J. Siteman Cancer Center">Alvin J. Siteman Cancer Center</a></li> 
      <li>Center for Advanced Medicine</li> 
      <li>Eric P. Newman Education Center (conference and convention center)</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_and_West_Campuses">North and West Campuses</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: North and West Campuses">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Washington University's North Campus and West Campus principally house administrative functions that are not student focused. North Campus lies in St. Louis City near the <a href="/wiki/Delmar_Loop" title="Delmar Loop">Delmar Loop</a>. The University acquired the building and adjacent property in 2004, formerly home to the Angelica Uniform Factory.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Several University administrative departments are located at the North Campus location, including offices for Quadrangle Housing, Accounting and Treasury Services, Parking and Transportation Services, <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Officer_Training_Corps" title="Reserve Officer Training Corps" class="mw-redirect">Army ROTC</a>, and Network Technology Services. The North Campus location also provides off-site storage space for the <a href="/wiki/Performing_arts" title="Performing arts">Performing Arts</a> Department. Renovations are still ongoing; recent additions to the North Campus space include a small eatery operated by <a href="/wiki/Bon_App%C3%A9tit_Management_Company" title="Bon App&eacute;tit Management Company">Bon App&eacute;tit Management Company</a>, the University's on-campus food provider, completed during spring semester 2007, as well as the Family Learning Center, operated by Bright Horizons and opened in September 2010.</p> 
     <p>The West Campus is located about one&nbsp;mile (1.6 km) to the west of the Danforth Campus in <a href="/wiki/Clayton,_Missouri" title="Clayton, Missouri">Clayton, Missouri</a>, and primarily consists of a three-story former <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department store</a> building housing mostly administrative space. The West Campus building was home to the Clayton branch of the <a href="/wiki/Famous-Barr" title="Famous-Barr">Famous-Barr</a> department store until 1990, when the University acquired the property and adjacent parking and began a series of renovations.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Today, the basement level houses the West Campus Library, the University Archives, the Modern Graphic History Library, and conference space. The ground level still remains a retail space. The upper floors house consolidated capital gifts, portions of alumni and development, and <a href="/wiki/Information_systems" title="Information systems">information systems</a> offices from across the Danforth and Medical School campuses. There is also a music rehearsal room on the second floor. The West Campus is also home to the Center for the Application of Information Technologies (CAIT), which provides IT training services.</p> 
     <p>Both the North and West Campuses are accessible by the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_MetroLink" title="St. Louis MetroLink" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis MetroLink</a>, which, with the <a href="/wiki/Delmar_Loop_(St._Louis_MetroLink)" title="Delmar Loop (St. Louis MetroLink)">Delmar Loop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forsyth_(St._Louis_MetroLink)" title="Forsyth (St. Louis MetroLink)">Forsyth MetroLink Stations</a> directly adjacent to these campuses, provides easy travel around the St. Louis metropolitan area, including all of Washington University's campuses.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tyson_Research_Center">Tyson Research Center</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tyson Research Center">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Tyson_Research_Center" title="Tyson Research Center">Tyson Research Center</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Tyson Research Center is a 2,000-acre (809&nbsp;ha) field station located west of <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri" class="mw-redirect">St. Louis</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Meramec_River" title="Meramec River">Meramec River</a>. Washington University obtained Tyson as surplus property from the federal government in 1963. It is used by the University as a biological field station and research/education center. In 2010 the Living Learning Center was named one of the first two buildings accredited nationwide as a &quot;living building&quot; under the Living Building Challenge,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> opened to serve as a biological research station and classroom for summer students.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Washington_University_in_St._Louis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2>
